Week 3 Notes¶

While loops¶

In [2]:
n=0
while n < 5:
    print(n)
    n += 1

0
1
2
3
4

Be careful to avoid run away loops.

while n < 5:
    print(n)

KeyboardInterrupt:

We want to do integer division to get the divisor and remainder.

In [11]:
def integer_division(m,n):  # inputs are integers m and n
    sum1 = 0                # storage variable for growing sum of divisors
    q = 0                   # storage variable for the quotient
    while sum1 <= m-n:      # loop runs until the sum exceeds m-n
        sum1 += n           # increment the sum by n
        q += 1              # increment the quotient by 1
    r = m - sum1            # calculate the remainder
    return (q,r)            # output is a tuple containing the quotient and remainder
In [9]:
integer_division(8,3)
Out[9]:
(2, 2)
In [12]:
integer_division(9,3)
Out[12]:
(3, 0)

List comprehension¶

In [13]:
list3 = []
for i in range(5):
    list3.append(3*i)
print(list3)

[0, 3, 6, 9, 12]

We can do the same thing with only one line of code.

In [14]:
list31 = [3*i for i in range(5)]
print(list31)

[0, 3, 6, 9, 12]

We can add conditions to the list.

In [16]:
list32 = [3*i for i in range(10) if i%2 != 0]
print(list32)

[3, 9, 15, 21, 27]
In [17]:
list33 = [3*i for i in range(20) if i%2 != 0 and i%3 != 0]
                            # list of numbers that have a single 
                                #factor of 3 and no factors of 2
print(list33)               # Prints the list

[3, 15, 21, 33, 39, 51, 57]

Plotting with Matplotlib¶

In [1]:
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
In [6]:
plt.plot([0,1,2,3],[1,2,4,8])    # basic plot command
plt.xlabel('$x$',fontsize=14)    # command to add a label to the x axis
plt.ylabel('$2^x$',fontsize=14)  # command to add a label to the y axis
plt.title('$y=2^x$')
plt.show()
In [7]:
inputs1 = [k for k in range(-5,6)]  # -5,-4,-3,...,3,4,5
outputs1 = [x**2 + 1 for x in inputs1]
In [10]:
plt.plot(inputs1,outputs1,'k-o')
plt.show()
In [11]:
inputs2 = [0.05*k for k in range(-100,101)]
outputs2 = [x**2 +1 for x in inputs2]
In [12]:
plt.plot(inputs1,outputs1,'ko')
plt.plot(inputs2,outputs2,'k-')
plt.show()
In [17]:
plt.figure(figsize=(10,4))
plt.subplot(1,2,1)
plt.plot(inputs1,outputs1,'k-o')
plt.xlabel('$x$')
plt.ylabel('$x^2+1$')
plt.title('$y=x^2+1$ with 11 points')
plt.subplot(1,2,2)
plt.plot(inputs1,outputs1,'ko')
plt.plot(inputs2,outputs2,'k-')
plt.xlabel('$x$')
plt.ylabel('$x^2+1$')
plt.title('$y=x^2+1$ with 201 points')
plt.show()

Plotting two functions¶

In [18]:
from math import e
In [19]:
outputs2exp = [e**x for x in inputs2]
In [27]:
plt.plot(inputs2,outputs2,'b-')     # Plot y=x^2+1
plt.plot(inputs2,outputs2exp,'r-')  # Plot y=e^x
plt.xlim([-5,5])
plt.ylim([0,25])
plt.legend(['$y=x^2+1$','$y=e^x$'],loc='upper center')
plt.show()

plt.legend has an optional argument for where you want the legend to be located, loc='location'. The default is 'best' which will place the legend in the "best location for you graph". You can also specify the location you want. Here are some of the options

  • loc = 'best' or loc=0
  • loc = 'upper right' or loc=1
  • loc = 'upper left' or loc=2
  • loc = 'upper center' or loc=9
In [44]:
plt.figure(figsize=(10,10))
plt.subplot(2,2,1)
plt.plot(inputs2,outputs2,'k-')
plt.title('solid black curve using k-')
plt.subplot(2,2,2)
plt.plot(inputs2,outputs2,'b--')
plt.title('dashed blue curve using b--')
plt.subplot(2,2,3)
plt.plot(inputs2,outputs2,'g:')
plt.title('dotted green curve using g:')
plt.subplot(2,2,4)
plt.plot(inputs2,outputs2,'r-.')
plt.title('red dash-dot curve using r-.')
plt.show()

Here are some more options for line and marker style:

Line colors

  • r for red
  • m for magenta
  • b for blue
  • k for black
  • c for cyan
  • g for green

Line styles

  • - for solid line
  • -- for dashed line
  • : for dotted line
  • -. for dash-dot line

Marker styles

  • . for points
  • o for solid circles
  • v for a down triangle
  • ^ for up triangle
  • * for stars

Exercise Plot one period of $ y =\sin(x) $, $y=\cos(x) $ on the same plot. Plot three periods $ y=\tan(x) $ along with dashed lines for the asymptotes.

In [30]:
from math import sin,pi
In [41]:
inputs=[2*pi*k/100 for k in range(101)]               # inputs for the function
outputs = [sin(x) for x in inputs]                    # outputs for the function
xtick_positions = [k*pi/2 for k in range(5)]          # positions along the x axis for the tick marks
xticks = ['0','$\pi/2$','$\pi$','$3\pi/2$','$2\pi$']  # labels for the x axis tick marks
ytick_positions = [0.5*k for k in range(-2,3)]        # positions along the y axis for the tick marks
yticks = ['-1','-1/2','0','1/2','1']                  # labels for the y axis tick marks
plt.plot(inputs,outputs)                              # plot the function
plt.xticks(xtick_positions,xticks)                    # generates the x tick marks
plt.yticks(ytick_positions,yticks)                    # generates the y tick marks
plt.show()
In [39]:
from math import tan
tan_inputs = [k*2*pi/200 for k in range(0,201)]
tan_outputs = [tan(x) for x in tan_inputs]
plt.plot(tan_inputs,tan_outputs)
plt.ylim([-5,5])                                  # Cut off the y values at 5
Out[39]:
(-5, 5)
In [ ]:
#  In the following cells, we will do the same thing as before, but this time we will break the input range up into 3 pieces
#  while avoiding the asymptotes.  This is accomplished by breaking the input list up into three.  There are two ways to
#  do this.
#
#  This is the first way.
tan_in1 = [k*pi/100 for k in range(0,50)]
tan_in2 = [k*pi/100 for k in range(51,150)]
tan_in3 = [k*pi/100 for k in range(151,200)]
In [ ]:
#  This is the second way
tan_inputs = [k*2*pi/200 for k in range(0,201)]
tan_in1 = [x for x in tan_inputs if x < pi/2]
tan_in2 = [x for x in tan_inputs if pi/2 < x and x < 3*pi/2]
tan_in3 = [x for x in tan_inputs if 3*pi/2 < x]
In [40]:
tan_out1 = [tan(x) for x in tan_in1]
tan_out2 = [tan(x) for x in tan_in2]
tan_out3 = [tan(x) for x in tan_in3]
plt.plot(tan_in1,tan_out1,'k-')
plt.plot(tan_in2,tan_out2,'k-')
plt.plot(tan_in3,tan_out3,'k-')
plt.plot([pi/2,pi/2],[-5,5],'r--')      # Produces the first asymptote at pi/2
plt.plot([3*pi/2,3*pi/2],[-5,5],'r--')  # Produces the second asymptote at 3pi/2
plt.ylim([-5,5])
plt.show()
In [ ]:
